Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and conditions for the opening takbir (takbir al-tahrimah). The commentary begins by asserting that the opening takbir is a condition (shart) for prayer, not a pillar (rukn), according to the majority of Hanafi scholars. Evidence is provided from the Quranic verse 'wa rabbaka fakabbir' interpreted as the opening takbir, and the hadith 'Miftah al-salah...' which shows that the takbir forbids worldly actions. The text then lists conditions for its validity: (1) no foreign action between intention and takbir, (2) performing it while standing before bowing, and (3) not delaying the intention after the takbir. The thamarah (practical consequence) of the dispute on whether it is a condition or pillar is explained: if one begins a fard prayer with takbir and then changes to nafl, according to the Hanafi view (shart) it is valid because a condition of fard can serve for nafl; according to the opposing view (rukn) it is invalid. The passage further discusses the validity of intention preceding the takbir, including the opinions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on intention made before entering the mosque, concluding that as long as one maintains the resolve, the intention is valid even if not consciously present at the takbir.

Key Terms
التحريمة (al-tahrimah): The opening takbir that makes previously permissible actions forbidden during prayer. It is a condition for prayer according to the Hanafi school.
النية (al-niyyah): The intention that must accompany the tahrimah, either simultaneously or preceding it without a foreign separator.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is valid because the takbir is a condition and can serve for nafl.

D) It is valid only if the person remains standing.

Key Takeaway
The opening takbir is a fard condition (shart) for prayer, not a pillar, and this classification affects the possibility of linking a nafl prayer to a fard intention.

Condition of No Interval Between Intention and Takbir and the Fruit of Disagreement
States the condition and thamarah of a cross-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi condition for the validity of the takbir (no foreign interval) and evaluate the disagreement about building nafil on fard takbir.

Core Idea
The takbir must follow intention without a break; Hanafis allow building nafil on fard's takbir because conditions, not pillars, are transferable.

This lesson examines a key condition for the validity of the opening takbir: it must be performed without any foreign action between the intention and the takbir. It then explores the practical consequence of this condition in a cross-madhhab dispute about whether a supererogatory prayer can be built upon the takbir of an obligatory prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
Recognize that the takbir (tahrimah) is valid only if there is no foreign interval between the intention and the takbir. The passage states this condition explicitly.
The interval is defined by actions that prevent the connection, like eating, drinking, and speaking.
2
Distinguish between breaking and non-breaking actions
Identify which actions break the connection (eating, drinking, speaking) and which do not (walking, wudu). The text provides this distinction based on consensus and reasoning.
Actions that are contrary to prayer break the connection; actions compatible with prayer do not.
3
Understand the fruit of disagreement (thamarah al-khilaf)
The dispute centers on whether the takbir of a fard prayer can serve as the base for a subsequent nafil prayer. The Hanafi position permits it; the opposing school (Shafi'i) does not.
The thamarah is the practical ruling: if one prays fard with the intention of nafil after takbir, Hanafi deems it valid, others invalid.
4
Compare proofs
The Hanafi proof: the condition of fard is valid as a condition for nafil, like all other conditions (e.g., purity, facing qibla). The Shafi'i proof: the takbir is a pillar of fard, and a pillar of fard cannot serve as a pillar for nafil.
Hanafi response: a condition (shart) is transferable, unlike a pillar (rukn).
5
Apply the rule to a scenario
Consider a person who intends to pray zuhr, then walks a few steps, then says takbir. According to Hanafi, walking does not break the connection, so the takbir is valid. If they drank water, it would break.
The hadith about Allah looking at hearts and intentions supports that contrary action indicates turning away from intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بلا فاصل، والإتيان بالتحريمةِ قائماً ، قبل انحنائه للركوع، وعدم تأخير النِّيَّةِ عن التَّحريمةِ . وثمرة الخلاف تظهر في جواز بناء النفل على تحريمة الفرض، فعندنا يجوز؛ لأن شرط الفرض يصلح شرطاً للنفل، كسائر الشروط، وعندهم لا يجوز، لأنها ركن الفرض، وركن الفرض وجزؤه لا يقع جزءاً من النفل، والشرط لصحة التحريمة أن توجد بلا فاصل بينها وبين النية بأجنبي يمنع الاتصال، للإجماع على ذلك، كالأكل والشرب والكلام، وأما المشي والوضوء فليسا مانعين. وقال في البنداري: لأنه بالعمل المنافي يصير تاركاً للنية معرضاً عنها، لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: إِنَّ اللَّهَ لا يَنْظُرُ إِلَى صُوَرِكُمْ وَأَمْوَالِكُمْ وَلَكِنْ يَنْظُرُ إِلَى قُلُوبِكُمْ وَنِيَّاتِكُمْ) كذا في ( مجمع الروايات».
Without an interval, and performing the tahrimah while standing before his bowing, and not delaying the intention after the tahrimah. The fruit of the disagreement appears in the permissibility of building a supererogatory prayer on the tahrimah of an obligatory prayer. With us it is permissible, because the condition of the obligatory prayer is valid as a condition for the supererogatory, like all other conditions. With them it is not permissible, because it is a pillar of the obligatory prayer, and a pillar or part of the obligatory prayer does not occur as part of the supererogatory. The condition for the validity of the tahrimah is that it exists without an interval between it and the intention caused by something extraneous that prevents connection, by consensus on that, like eating, drinking, and speaking. As for walking and wudu, they are not preventers. The author of al-Bandari said: because by contrary action he becomes a leaver of the intention and turning away from it, due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'Indeed Allah does not look at your forms and wealth, but He looks at your hearts and intentions.' Thus in Majma' al-Riwayat.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the condition of no foreign action between the intention and the tahrimah (opening takbir), and the practical consequence (thamarah) of that condition. The Hanafi position is that the tahrimah must be free of any interval caused by an extraneous action that breaks the connection between intention and takbir. Actions like eating, drinking, and speaking break the connection, while walking or performing wudu do not. The fruit of the disagreement is whether the takbir of a fard prayer can serve as the basis for a subsequent nafil prayer. Hanafis permit it because the conditions are the same; the opposing school (likely Shafi'i) prohibits it because they consider the takbir a pillar specific to the fard. The passage cites a hadith about Allah looking at hearts and intentions as a proof that contrary action indicates turning away from the intention.

Key Terms
تحريمة (tahrimah): The opening takbir (saying Allahu Akbar) that commences the prayer
فاصل (fasil): An interval or foreign action that breaks the connection between intention and takbir
نفل (nafl): Supererogatory prayer
شرط (shart): Condition, something whose presence is necessary for validity but is not part of the essence
ركن (rukn): Pillar, an integral part of the act without which the act is invalid

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following actions between intention and takbir would break the connection and invalidate the takbir?
A) Walking to the prayer mat
B) Performing wudu
C) Drinking water
D) Adjusting one's clothing

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits building a supererogatory prayer on the takbir of an obligatory prayer because conditions are transferable, while the Shafi'i school prohibits it because the takbir is a pillar specific to the fard.

Condition of Standing for the Opening Takbir
Stating the condition that tahrimah must be performed while standing before bowing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition that the tahrimah must be performed while closer to standing than to bowing, and apply it to the scenario of catching the imam in ruku.

Core Idea
For the opening takbir to be valid, you must be closer to standing than to bowing — otherwise your prayer does not begin.

The opening takbir (tahrimah) is the act that formally begins the prayer. Its validity depends on certain conditions, one of which is the posture of standing. This lesson clarifies what 'standing' means before the takbir, particularly when joining the imam who is already in ruku, and addresses a subtle issue about intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
The passage states that the tahrimah must be performed while standing and before completing the bowing motion. The key phrase is 'قائماً قبل تمام انحنائه' (standing before completion of his bowing).
This is one of eleven conditions for the validity of the tahrimah.
2
Determine the criterion
The measure is proximity: you must be closer to standing (أقرب إلى القيام) than to ruku (أقرب إلى الركوع) at the moment of the takbir.
If equidistant, the Hanafi view is that it is invalid because the condition is not fully met.
3
Apply to the mas'ala of catching the imam in ruku
If you catch the imam in ruku, you bend your back. Assess your angle: if you are still closer to standing when you say the takbir, you enter prayer; if closer to ruku, you do not.
This is a common practical scenario for latecomers.
4
Analyze the intention scenario
If you say the takbir while standing but intend it as the takbir for ruku (i.e., for moving into ruku), the prayer still starts. The intention is nullified because the takbir itself is for entering prayer.
The legal effect of the takbir (entering prayer) overrides the specific intention.
5
Note the ikhtilaf
Some scholars (خلافاً لبعضهم) disagree on this intention point. The Hanafi view is that the prayer begins regardless, as the takbir's function is to commence prayer.
This is a legitimate difference of opinion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

It is required for the validity of the tahrimah eleven conditions, five of which I mentioned in the matn, and the rest in the sharh. One of them: (performing the tahrimah while standing before) completion (of his bowing) such that he is not closer (to ruku). Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement enumerating the conditions for the validity of the tahrimah (opening takbir). It specifies that one must be standing before bowing when saying the takbir. The key measure is whether one is closer to standing or to ruku (bowing). An example is given of joining the imam who is already in ruku: if the person bends but is still closer to standing when saying takbir, the prayer begins; if closer to ruku, it does not. A secondary point addresses a rare situation: if a person says takbir while standing but intends it as the takbir for ruku (i.e., he thinks he is doing the ruku takbir), the prayer still starts because the takbir itself is for entering prayer, not for ruku. This view contradicts some scholars.

قائم (qa'im): Standing upright

الركوع (ruku): Bowing position of prayer

Timing of Intention for the Opening Takbir
Explains the ruling that intention must not be delayed, with intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for the timing of intention in relation to the opening takbir and distinguish it from the positions of al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad.

Core Idea
Intention may precede the takbir as long as no foreign action breaks the connection.

The timing of intention (niyyah) relative to the opening takbir (tahrimah) is a core difference between the Hanafi school and others. The Hanafi school holds that intention does not need to be simultaneous; it may precede the takbir as long as the worshiper remains committed and does not engage in any act contrary to prayer. This lesson examines the evidence, the intra-Hanafi views, and the inter-madhhab disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and its scope
The passage states: 'The second condition for the validity of the tahrimah is not delaying the intention after the tahrimah.' Understand that this condition allows both simultaneity and precedence, but prohibits delay.
The key is that the intention must be present at or before the takbir, not after.
2
Understand the Hanafi ruling on preceded intention
The text explains that a preceded intention is valid as long as no foreign interval (fāṣil ajnabī) occurs. This includes actions like walking, eating, or speaking that break the continuity of the intention.
The intention is considered 'like the one present at the takbir' if nothing cuts the commitment.
3
Contrast with the positions of other schools
Al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad require simultaneity (muqāranah ḥaqīqiyyah) – intention must be present exactly at the takbir. Dawud al-Zahiri requires intention to precede. Note the thamarah al-khilaf: a person who intends before but forgets at the takbir has a valid prayer in Hanafi but invalid in Shafi'i.
The Hanafi response emphasizes that the quality of connection is not broken by mere passage of time if the person remains resolved.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Within the Hanafi school, al-Karkhi and al-Tahawi allowed delayed intention (after takbir) up to certain points (ta'awwudh, ruku, etc.), analogizing to fasting. However, the text rejects this analogy because fasting has a hardship that prayer does not.
The mufta bihi (relied-upon position) is that delayed intention is invalid; preceded intention is valid.
5
Conclude with the mufta bihi
The text explicitly states that the 'apparent riwayah' (zāhir al-riwāyah) is that the delayed intention is not considered. Thus, the practical ruling for fatwa is that intention must not be delayed after the takbir, but it may precede as long as no foreign action intervenes.
This is the position taken by the majority of Hanafi jurists.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the timing of intention in relation to the opening takbir. The Hanafi position is that the intention must not be delayed after the takbir; it can be simultaneous or even precede the takbir, as long as no foreign action breaks the connection. The best is simultaneity to avoid disagreement. The text reports various Hanafi views: Abu Hanifa allows the intention to be made even before the prayer time, as long as the person does not engage in contrary actions (like eating, speaking). This is supported by narrations from Abu Yusuf: if one leaves home intending to pray with the congregation and upon arrival says takbir without recalling the intention, the prayer is valid because the intention persists. The text contrasts the Hanafi view with that of al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad, who require intention to be simultaneous with the takbir (neither before nor after), and Dawud al-Zahiri who requires intention before the takbir. The Hanafi school rejects the delayed intention (after takbir) as invalid in the apparent riwayah, though al-Karkhi allows it up to certain points analogizing to fasting, but the text clarifies that the analogy is flawed due to hardship in fasting.

Key Terms
نية (niyyah): The intention to perform a specific prayer
مقارنة حقيقية (muqaranah haqiqiyyah): True simultaneity between intention and takbir
نيـة متقدمة (niyyah mutaqaddimah): Intention made before the takbir

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a preceded intention for prayer is valid as long as no foreign action intervenes, while a delayed intention is invalid — this differs from the Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali requirement of simultaneity.

Condition of Audible Pronunciation of the Opening Takbir
Condition of Audible Pronunciation for the Opening Takbir and Exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of audible pronunciation for the opening takbir, identify the exceptions (mute, illiterate), and apply the ruling to other verbal acts in worship and worldly matters.

Core Idea
The opening takbir must be spoken so you hear it – mere tongue movement without sound is invalid.

In this lesson, we examine a key condition for the validity of the opening takbir: the worshipper must pronounce it audibly enough that he can hear himself. The passage also addresses exceptions for those unable to speak or articulate, and clarifies that this condition applies to all verbal elements of prayer and other legal acts. Understanding this condition helps avoid common errors in salah and other rituals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Condition
The act is the opening takbir (tahrimah). The third condition is 'al-nutq bi al-tahrimah' – pronouncing it so that one hears oneself, assuming no deafness.
This is a shart (condition) for validity, not a rukn (pillar) in itself.
2
Define Audibility: Jahr versus Mukhaftah
Jahr (loud) means making others hear; mukhaftah (quiet) means making oneself hear. The required level for the takbir is mukhaftah – the person must hear his own pronunciation.
Al-Hinduwani adds that the ears must hear it and even those nearby should hear it, but the majority requires only self-hearing.
3
Recognize the Exceptions
The mute (akhras) and illiterate (ummi) who cannot articulate are excused; their intention suffices for starting the prayer. The mute is not required to move his tongue.
This is because they do the utmost of their ability (maqsura).
4
Extend the Condition to Other Verbal Acts
The same condition applies to recitation, tashahhud, dhikr, tasmiyah on slaughter, sajdah tilawah, divorce, oaths, manumission, etc. If any of these are expressed silently without audible sound, they are not valid.
Kamal ibn al-Humam argues that letters without sound are not actual speech.
5
Distinguish Intention from Pronunciation
The intention is a heart act and does not require pronunciation. The scholars unanimously agree that intention without verbal expression is valid, though speaking it is recommended to gather resolve.
This is a crucial distinction: the condition of audibility applies to the takbir and other verbal acts, but not to the niyyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The mute is not required to move his tongue according to the correct view. The correct view is that jahr (loud) in reality is to make others hear, and mukhaftah (quiet) is to make oneself hear. This is the statement of al-Hinduwani: he must make himself hear. Al-Mujtaba added in the transmission from al-Hinduwani that it does not suffice unless his own ears hear it and those near him. End. Even if one executes divorce in his heart and moves his tongue without audible articulation, it does not occur. If one corrects the letters and moves them, al-Karkhi said: recitation is correcting the letters, even if there is no sound that can be heard. The investigator Kamal ibn al-Humam said: Know that recitation, although it is an action of the tongue, but its action is speech, and speech is with letters, and letters are qualities that occur to sound, and sound is more specific than breath. So mere correction of letters without sound is an indication of letters by the muscles of the articulation points, not actual letters, so it is not speech. End. A note: Our requirement of pronouncing the tahrimah indicates that pronouncing the intention is not required, because it pertains to the heart, for which pronunciation is not required. The scholars have unanimously agreed that if one intends with his heart and does not speak his intention, it is permissible. In al-Diraya from al-Mabsut: speaking the intention is not considered, but if one does it to gather the resolve of his heart, it is good. Al-Tahawi and Qadi Khan said: the intention along with verbal remembrance is better, because verbal remembrance establishes what...
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage states the condition that the opening takbir must be pronounced audibly enough that the person can hear themselves, unless there is an impediment (like deafness). The text discusses exceptions: a mute person or someone who cannot articulate properly may use intention. For those who cannot focus their heart on the intention, moving the tongue suffices. The majority Hanafi view requires the person to hear themselves; the definition of 'jahr' (loud) and 'mukhaftah' (quiet) is clarified: loud means others hear, quiet means the person hears themselves. This condition extends to all acts of speech in worship: recitation, tashahhud, dhikr, etc., and even to worldly matters like divorce and oaths. A notable proof from Kamal ibn al-Humam: mere correction of letters without sound is not speech. Importantly, the condition of audible pronunciation does not apply to the intention, which is a heart act. The intention is valid silently, though combining it with verbal mention is better.

Key Terms
النطق (nuta): Audible pronunciation, producing sound that one can hear
الأخرس (al-akhras): A mute person who cannot speak
الجهر (jahr): Loud pronunciation, making others hear
المخافتة (mukhaftah): Quiet pronunciation, making oneself hear

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The opening takbir must be pronounced audibly enough that the person can hear himself; this condition extends to all verbal acts in prayer and beyond, but not to intention.

Pronunciation Condition for Takbirat al-Ihram
Explains the condition of audible pronunciation in opening takbir and extends to all verbal acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of audible pronunciation in the opening takbir, identify exceptions, and apply the ruling to verbal acts in fiqh.

Core Idea
The opening takbir must be pronounced audibly to oneself; intention is a heart action and need not be spoken.

In the Hanafi school, the opening takbir is a pillar of prayer, but its validity depends on a condition: the worshipper must pronounce it audibly enough to hear himself. This lesson explores the rationale, exceptions, and extension to other verbal acts, and distinguishes the intention which is purely in the heart.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
The third condition of the opening takbir is that it must be pronounced such that the person can hear himself under normal circumstances. This is the minimum requirement.
The condition applies to all verbal acts, not just the takbir.
2
Recognize exceptions
The mute and illiterate are excused because they cannot fulfill the condition; their intention suffices to commence prayer. The mute is not even required to move his tongue.
This is based on the principle of 'taklif ma la yutaq' (obligation beyond capacity is not required).
3
Understand the extension
The condition of audible pronunciation applies to all verbal acts in fiqh: qira'ah, tashahhud, dhikr, tasmiyah on slaughter, sajdat al-tilawa, divorce, manumission, oaths, etc. Mere movement of the tongue without sound does not constitute valid speech.
This is the relied-upon position of the majority of mashayikh.
4
Analyze the rational proof
Ibn al-Humam argues that speech consists of letters, and letters are a quality affecting sound. Correcting the letters without voice is not speech but a gesture. Therefore, audible sound is necessary.
This proof refutes al-Karakhi's view that correcting letters suffices.
5
Distinguish from intention
Intention (niyyah) is a heart action; verbalization is not required and there is consensus on this. The condition of pronunciation applies only to verbal acts, not to the intention for prayer.
This distinction is highlighted in the concluding note of the passage.

Source Text

And the third condition of the opening takbir: (pronouncing the takbir such that he can hear himself) if he were not deaf. As for the mute and the illiterate person who does not know anything, their commencement (of prayer) is valid with the intention alone because they have done the utmost of what they are capable, and the mute is not required to move his tongue according to the correct view. In Al-Mujtaba and others: Whoever is unable to bring presence of heart in the intention or doubts about the intention, the tongue suffices him. Since the pronunciation of the takbir such that he hears himself is a matter related to the tongue, we say that it is required (according to the most correct view) as stated by Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani and most of the mashayikh. The correct view is that jahr (loud recitation) in reality means making others hear, and mukhafatah (audible to oneself) means making oneself hear, which is the statement of al-Hindwani: he said: It is necessary that he hears himself. And in Al-Mujtaba, in the transmission from al-Hindwani, that it does not suffice unless his ears hear it and those near him. End quote. And in Al-Dhakhira, from Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani that the most correct is this. And this condition applies to everything related to speech, such as the takbir, recitation, tashahhud, dhikrs, saying Bismillah on the slaughtered animal, the obligation of sajdat al-tilawa, manumission, divorce, exception (istithna'), oaths, and vows. So much so that if he performs divorce in his heart and moves his tongue without audible utterance, it does not occur. And if he corrects the letters and moves (the tongue), al-Karakhi said: Recitation is correcting the letters, even if there is no voice audible to oneself. And the muhaqqiq Kamal ibn al-Humam (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Know that recitation, although it is an action of the tongue, its action is speech, and speech is with letters, and letters are a quality that affects the voice, and it is more specific than breath. For the breath that is struck produces sound, and the letter is an accident in the sound, not in the breath. So mere correction of the letters (i.e., the letters) without voice is a gesture toward the letters with the muscles of the makharij, not letters, so it is not speech. End quote. Note: In our stipulating the pronunciation of the takbir is an indication that pronunciation of the intention is not required, because it (intention) is among the matters of the heart for which pronunciation is not required. And the scholars have unanimously agreed that if he intends with his heart and does not speak his intention, it is valid.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof discussing the third condition of the opening takbir: the takbir must be pronounced audibly such that the person can hear himself under normal circumstances. The text clarifies exceptions: a mute person or an illiterate person who cannot recite is excused and may commence prayer with intention alone. It then extends this condition to all verbal acts in fiqh, such as divorce, manumission, and oaths, stating that mere movement of the tongue without audible sound does not constitute valid speech. The author cites the views of al-Hindwani and others to specify that 'hearing oneself' is the minimum. A rational proof is presented through Ibn al-Humam's analysis that correct articulation of letters without sound is not speech. Finally, a note emphasizes that this condition does not apply to intention, which is an act of the heart, and there is consensus that verbalizing intention is not required.

Key Terms
تحريمة (tahrimah): The opening takbir (saying Allahu Akbar) that initiates the prayer.
مخافتة (mukhafatah): Recitation audible only to oneself, as opposed to jahr (loud).
نية (niyyah): Intention in the heart to perform a specific prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He must move his tongue even if no sound comes out.
B) He is excused and may commence prayer with intention alone.
C) He must whisper the takbir in his heart.
D) He must say it with his hand gestures.

Key Takeaway
The pronunciation of the opening takbir must be audible to oneself; this condition applies to all verbal acts, but intention is an exception.

Muqarana: Is Simultaneous Intention Required in Takbir?
Discusses whether intention must accompany the opening takbir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on muqarana, distinguish it from the Shafi'i position, and apply the thamarah al-khilaf in practice.

Core Idea
Muqarana is not a condition in Hanafi; intention can precede the takbir.

In prayer, the intention (niyyah) is essential. But must it coincide exactly with the opening takbir? This lesson examines the inter-madhhab dispute, focusing on why the Hanafi school relaxes this requirement while the Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools insist on strict simultaneity. Understanding this difference helps you apply the correct ruling in your own practice.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the core Hanafi view: intention can be mixed with takbir or even precede it; muqarana is not a necessary condition.
The text cites al-Tahawi and al-Karakhi as representatives.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
List the opposing schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali, Dawud) and their stricter requirements: some require strict simultaneity, others require intention before takbir.
Dawud al-Zahiri requires intention to precede; the others require it at the exact moment.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the analogy (qiyas) to fasting: if intention can be delayed in fasting due to hardship, why not in prayer? This is the Karakhi position.
Al-Karakhi allowed intention after takbir by analogy with fasting.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response rejects the analogy: the hardship in observing dawn (fasting) is absent in prayer, so the analogy fails. Hence the condition of continuity is not dropped.
The text says: 'لا حرج في الصلاة' – no hardship in prayer.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: a Hanafi can form intention just before takbir, while other schools require exact simultaneity. This affects how one prepares for prayer.
The mufta bihi is that intention after takbir is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنطق بالتحريمة، بحيث يُسْمِعُ نفسه، على الأصح.. الطحاوي والكرخي أنه يكبر تكبيرة مخالطة بالنية، ليس بشرط لازم. كذا في «الإيضاح». ويقول الطحاوي: قال الشافعي ومالك وأحمد: حتى لا يجوز بنية متقدمة ولا متأخرة، وقال داود: يجب أن يقدم النية على التكبير. انتهى. وأشرنا إلى أنه لا عبرة بالنية المتأخرة عن التحريمة، وهـو « ظـاهـر الـروايـة». وعن الكرخي: أنها تعتبر، واختلفوا على قوله، فقيل: إلى التعوذ، وقيل: إلى الركوع، وقيل: إلى الرفــع منه، قياساً على نية الصوم. والفرق بينهما على قولنا أن وصف الاتصال في الصوم ساقط للحرج، لأن ملاحظة حال طلوع الفجر عَسر، وفيه حرج، ولا حرج في الصلاة كما في « الدراية» وغيرها.
And the pronunciation of the takbir such that he hears himself, according to the most correct view. Al-Tahawi and al-Karakhi said: He says the takbir mixed with the intention; it is not a necessary condition. Thus in Al-Idah. And al-Tahawi said: Al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad said: It is not permissible with an intention that precedes or follows. And Dawud said: The intention must precede the takbir. End quote. And we indicated that no consideration is given to an intention that comes after the tahrimah, and this is Zahir al-Riwayah. And from al-Karakhi: That it (the intention) is considered (even if delayed), and they differed on his statement: some said until the ta'awwudh, some said until the ruku', and some said until rising from ruku', by analogy with the intention for fasting. And the difference between them according to our opinion is that the condition of continuity in fasting is dropped due to hardship, because observing the time of dawn is difficult and there is hardship, and there is no hardship in prayer, as in Al-Dirayah and others.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning whether the intention (niyyah) must be exactly simultaneous (muqarana) with the opening takbir. The Hanafi position, represented by al-Tahawi and al-Karakhi, is that mixing the intention with the takbir is not a necessary condition; the intention can precede the takbir. The opposing schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) require strict concurrency, and Dawud al-Zahiri requires the intention to precede. The text then notes that Zahir al-Riwayah rejects an intention that comes after the takbir. However, al-Karakhi's alternate view allows a delayed intention by analogy with fasting, but the text refutes this by highlighting the hardship in fasting that is absent in prayer. Thus, the Hanafi position prefers allowing the intention to precede or be mixed, but not to follow.

Key Terms
مقارنة (muqarana): The condition that the intention coincides exactly with the opening takbir.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The well-known transmitted position of the Hanafi school from the six books of Muhammad al-Shaybani.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning used to extend a ruling from a known case (fasting) to a new case (prayer).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, is it permissible for the intention to be made slightly before the opening takbir?
A) No, it must be exactly at the same moment.
B) Yes, it is permissible; muqarana is not a necessary condition.
C) Yes, but only for the imam.
D) No, it must be after the takbir.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the intention may precede or be mixed with the opening takbir; it is not required to be simultaneous.

Ruling on Verbalizing the Intention (Talfiz bi al-Niyyah)
Intra-Hanafi dispute on verbalizing intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the differing Hanafi views on talfiz bi al-niyyah and apply the recommended supplication format in practice.

Core Idea
Verbalizing intention is not a Prophetic sunna; the recommended wording is a supplication, not a declaration.

The issue of saying the intention aloud before prayer (talfiz bi al-niyyah) has been debated among Hanafi scholars. Some view it as a sunna, others as a bid'ah. This passage from Imdad al-Fattah explores these views and provides guidance on the proper manner of verbalizing intention, emphasizing a supplicatory formula rather than a statement of intent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its disputed status
What is talfiz bi al-niyyah? It is speaking the intention before prayer. Determine the different legal rulings assigned: some say sunna, some say mubah (permissible), some say bid'ah.
The act is not part of the prayer itself but a preceding action.
2
Trace the proof for each position
Proponents of sunna (Muhammad, Tahawi, Qadi Khan) argue that mentioning with the tongue strengthens the heart's intention. Critics (Ibn al-Humam quoting Ibn Qayyim) say there is no authentic report from the Prophet or Companions saying 'I intend'; the Prophet only said the takbir.
The dalil for the critics is the absence of evidence; the proponents rely on rationality (istihsan).
3
Analyze the reconciliation
The text clarifies that those who call it sunna mean it is a practice of later mashayikh (scholars), not a sunna of the Prophet. This resolves the apparent contradiction.
The dispute is partly terminological: what 'sunna' means.
4
Extract the recommended practice
The text advises against saying 'nawaytu' (I intended) because it can be a lie or disrespectful. Instead, say a supplication: 'Allahumma inni uridu an usalliya... fayassirha li wa taqabbalha minni' (O Allah, I desire to pray... so make it easy and accept it).
This is based on a report from Muhammad regarding hajj, but the text notes prayer is brief so the supplication is not prescribed, yet still allowed.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that verbalizing intention is not a Prophetic sunna. It is permissible quietly if done as a du'a, but not with 'nawaytu'. The majority of later Hanafis adopt this view.
The text leans towards limiting verbalization and using du'a format.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الدراية» عن «المبسوط : التكلم بالنية لا يعتبر به، وإن فعله ليجمع عزيمة قلبـه فحسن. وقال الطحاوي و « قاضي خان : القصد مع الذكر باللسان أفضل، لأن الذكر باللسان يقرر ما القلب ويؤكده، وذلك أن السنة الوسوسة، وعمره إنما زجر من جهر به، فأما المخافتة بها فلا بأس كذا في جوامع الأحكام ولوامع الإلهام انتهى. فمن قال: إن التلفظ بالنية سنّةٌ، لم يُرد به كونه سنة النبي، بل سنة بعض المشايخ، اختاروه لاختلاف الزمان وكثرة الشواغل على القلوب فيما بعد زمن الصحابة والتابعين. عمر تنبيه آخر في كيفية التلفظ بها : قال في ( الدراية » : لا يقول: نويت كذا لأنه يكون كذباً إن لم يكن نوى، ويقع إخباراً عن المحقق إن كان نوى من غير حاجة، ولكن يقول: اللهم إني أريد أن أصلي كذا فيسرها لي وتقبلها مني، كما ورد عن محمد في إحرام الحج. انتهى. وفي « المبسوط » : لا ينبغي أن يقول: نويت لأنه لو لم ينو، فقد كذب، وإن نوى بعد النية، فقد أخبر الله تعالى بما في ضميره، مع أنه تعالى عالم به، فيكون مستفهماً، بل يقول: اللهم إني أريد أن أصلي صلاة كذا، فيسرها لي، وتقبلها مني، ليكون دعاء بالقبول وطلباً للتيسير من الله تعالى، في حصول مقصوده. وكذا ذكر القاضي صدر الإسلام في مبسوطه»، والقاضي بدر الأئمة، كذلك قال في «مجمع الروايات»، وكذا نقله في ( البحر ) عن ( المحيط ) ، ثم قال : وهذا كله يفيد أن التلفظ بها يكون بهذه العبارة: اللهم كتاب الحج، أن الحج لما كان يمتد ويقع فيه العوارض والموانع وهو عبادة عظيمة يحصل بأفعال شاقة، استحب طلب التيسير والتسهيل من الله تعالى، ولم يشرع مثل هذا الدعاء في الصلاة، لأن أداءها في وقت يسير. انتهى. وهو صريح في نفي قياس الصلاة (1) أخرجه البخاري في الأذان، باب التكبير إذا قام من السجود (۷۸۹) ، ومسلم في الصلاة، باب: التكبير في كــل خفض ورفع (۳۹۲). - ۲۲۱ -
And in Al-Diraya from Al-Mabsut: Speaking the intention is not taken into account, but if he does it to gather his resolve, it is good. Al-Tahawi and Qadi Khan said: The intent along with mentioning with the tongue is better, because mentioning with the tongue strengthens the heart and confirms it, and that is because the sunna was legislated to complete the obligation, and mentioning it reinforces the obligation, so it becomes a sunna. End quote. And in Al-Ikhtiyar Sharh al-Mukhtar, Muhammad ibn al-Hasan said: Intention in the heart is fard, and mentioning it with the tongue is sunna, and combining both is best. End quote. Kamal ibn al-Humam said: Some hadith scholars, meaning Ibn Qayyim al-Jawziyya, as appears from his words, may Allah have mercy on him: It is not established from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) by an authentic or weak chain that he used to say at the opening 'I pray such and such', nor from any of the Companions or Successors, rather the transmitted report is that 'when he stood for prayer, he would say the takbir' - this is an innovation. End quote. And in Majma' al-Riwayat: Some scholars disliked verbalizing the intention, because it is against proper conduct, and some permitted it because it actualizes the heart's action and cuts off waswasa. Umar (ra) only forbade doing it loudly, but as for speaking softly, there is no harm. Thus in Jami' al-Ahkam and Lawami' al-Ilham. So whoever says that verbalizing the intention is sunna does not mean it is a sunna of the Prophet, but rather a sunna of some mashayikh, who chose it due to the change of times and the many preoccupations of the hearts after the era of the Companions and Successors. Another note on the manner of verbalizing: He said in Al-Diraya: He should not say 'I intended such and such' because it would be a lie if he had not intended, and it becomes a report of what is realized if he did intend without need. Rather, he should say: 'O Allah, I desire to pray such and such, so make it easy for me and accept it from me', as reported from Muhammad concerning the ihram of Hajj. End quote. And in Al-Mabsut: He should not say 'I intended' because if he did not intend, he has lied, and if he intends after the intention, he has informed Allah of what is in his heart, while Allah is knowing of it, so he becomes disrespectful. Rather, he should say: 'O Allah, I desire to pray such and such, so make it easy for me and accept it from me' so that it is a supplication for acceptance and a request for facilitation from Allah. Similarly, al-Qadi Sadr al-Islam mentioned in his Mabsut, and al-Qadi Badr al-A'imma, likewise said in Majma' al-Riwayat, and it is transmitted in Al-Bahr from Al-Muhit, then he said: All this indicates that verbalizing should be with this expression: 'O Allah, I desire...' not with 'I intended' or 'I intend', as is the practice of the common people and others. It is not hidden that asking for success and acceptance is something other than verbalizing it. However, more than one of our mashayikh have mentioned regarding what Muhammad said in the book of Hajj, that since Hajj is lengthy and involves obstacles and impediments, and is a great worship involving difficult actions, seeking ease and facilitation from Allah is recommended. But such a supplication is not prescribed for prayer, because its performance takes a short time. End quote. And this is explicit in negating analogy with prayer. (1) Narrated by Bukhari in Adhan, chapter on Takbir when rising from prostration (789), and Muslim in Salah, chapter on Takbir in every bowing and rising (392). -221-
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling on verbalizing the intention (talfiz bi al-niyyah) before prayer. It presents multiple opinions: some Hanafi scholars (Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, Tahawi, Qadi Khan) consider it a sunna or mustahabb, while others, including Ibn al-Humam quoting Ibn Qayyim, consider it an innovation (bid'ah) because it is not proven from the Prophet. The text reconciles by stating that those who call it sunna mean it is a recommended practice of later scholars, not a Prophetic sunna. It also provides guidance on the proper wording: one should not say 'I intended' but rather a supplication asking Allah to make it easy and accept it. This is based on a report from Muhammad regarding hajj, but the text clarifies that such a supplication is not prescribed for prayer because prayer is brief. Thus, the relied-upon view is that verbalizing intention is not a Prophetic sunna but may be done quietly to overcome waswasa, perhaps using a supplication format.

Key Terms
التلفظ بالنية (al-talfiz bi al-niyyah): The act of speaking the intention aloud before beginning prayer.
بدعة (bid'ah): An innovation in religion, here referring to saying the intention aloud without Prophetic basis.
دعاء (du'a'): Supplication; the recommended wording for verbalizing intention is a prayer for acceptance, not a declaration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the recommended wording for verbalizing the intention according to the text?
A) 'I intend to pray fajr.'
B) 'O Allah, I desire to pray fajr, so make it easy for me and accept it.'
C) 'I am praying fajr for Allah.'
D) 'There is no god but Allah.'

Key Takeaway
Verbalizing intention is not a Prophetic sunna; the recommended practice is to say a supplication asking Allah for ease and acceptance, not 'I intend'.

Vocalizing the Intention (Talfuz bi-Niyyah): Sunna or Practice of the Later Scholars?
Discussion of the ruling and proper phrasing of vocalized intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on vocalizing the intention in prayer, distinguish between prophetic sunna and the practice of later mashayikh, and identify the correct supplicatory phrasing.

Core Idea
Vocalizing the intention is not from the Prophet but a later practice; the heart intention is fard, the tongue mention is sunna only as a means.

This lesson examines the disputed issue of vocalizing the intention (talfuz bi-niyyah) before prayer. While the Hanafi school clearly holds that intention in the heart is obligatory (fard), there is internal difference on whether saying it aloud is recommended (sunna), permissible, or even an innovation (bid'ah). The passage synthesizes these views and also prescribes a specific supplicatory phrasing to avoid potential false reports. By the end, you will be able to navigate this nuance and apply the mufta bihi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is vocalizing the intention (talfuz bi-niyyah) before prayer. According to Imam Muhammad, intention by heart (niyyah bi'l-qalb) is fard, and mentioning it with the tongue (dhikruha bi'l-lisan) is sunna. Combining both is best.
This is the primary Hanafi position from Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Locate the dalil and its implication
The dalil is the hadith that the Prophet (peace be upon him) when starting prayer would say the takbir—not 'I pray such-and-such'. This absence of verbal intention in prophetic practice is used by critics (like Ibn al-Humam and Ibn Qayyim) to argue that vocalizing is a bid'ah.
The hadith is sahih; the argument is from silence.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah and the Hanafi response
The wajh al-dalalah is that the Prophet's established practice (sunna fi'liyya) did not include verbal intention. Hanafi scholars reconcile this by asserting that vocalizing is not a prophetic sunna but a 'sunna of the mashayikh'—a later practice adopted due to changing times and distractions.
This is a case of istihsan or maslaha overriding strict analogy.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and its resolution
Al-Kamal ibn al-Humam and Ibn Qayyim considered it bid'ah; others (Majma' al-Riwayat) permitted it softly. The mufta bihi view is that vocalizing is permissible and may be recommended (sunnah ghayr muakkadah), but the phrasing must be supplicatory, not declaratory.
The phrasing should be: 'Allahumma inni uridu an usalliya...' not 'Nawaytu...'.
5
Evaluate the practical ruling
The operative ruling for fatwa: vocalizing intention is not required but allowed; it is better to phrase it as a supplication for acceptance and facilitation, avoiding 'nawaytu'. This applies only to prayer? The text notes that for Hajj and possibly fasting, supplication is recommended due to length and hardship.
Some later Hanafi authorities extend this supplication to all acts of worship.

القلب ويؤكده، وذلك أن السنة شرعت لإكمال الفرض، والذكر به مؤكد للفرض، فيكون سنة. انتهى. وفي ( الاختيار شرح المختار»، قال محمد بن الحسن النية بالقلب فرض، وذكرها باللسان سنة، والجمع بينهما أفضل، انتهى. وقال الكمال بن الهمام: قال بعض الحفاظ، يعني بـه ابـن قـيـم الجوزية، كما أفاده من لفظه رحمه الله : لم يثبت عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بطريق صحيح، ولا ضعيف أنه كان يقول عند الافتتاح أصلي كذا، ولا عن أحد من الصحابة والتابعين، بل المنقول « أَنَّهُ كَانَ ، إذا قام إلى الصَّلاةِ كَبَّرَ » (١) ، وهذه بدعة. انتهى. وفي «مجمع الروايات»: التلفظ بالنية كَرَّهَهُ البعض، لأن ـره أدب من فعله، وأباحه بعض لما فيه من تحقيق عمل القلب وقطع الوسوسة، وعمره إنما زجر من جهر به، فأما المخافتة بها فلا بأس كذا في جوامع الأحكام ولوامع الإلهام انتهى. فمن قال: إن التلفظ بالنية سنّةٌ، لم يُرد به كونه سنة النبي، بل سنة بعض المشايخ، اختاروه لاختلاف الزمان وكثرة الشواغل على القلوب فيما بعد زمن الصحابة والتابعين.

تنبيه آخر في كيفية التلفظ بها : قال في ( الدراية » : لا يقول: نويت كذا لأنه يكون كذباً إن لم يكن نوى، ويقع إخباراً عن المحقق إن كان نوى من غير حاجة، ولكن يقول: اللهم إني أريد أن أصلي كذا فيسرها لي وتقبلها مني، كما ورد عن محمد في إحرام الحج. انتهى. وفي « المبسوط » : لا ينبغي أن يقول: نويت لأنه لو لم ينو، فقد كذب، وإن نوى بعد النية، فقد أخبر الله تعالى بما في ضميره، مع أنه تعالى عالم به، فيكون مستفهماً، بل يقول: اللهم إني أريد أن أصلي صلاة كذا، فيسرها لي، وتقبلها مني، ليكون دعاء بالقبول وطلباً للتيسير من الله تعالى، في حصول مقصوده. وكذا ذكر القاضي صدر الإسلام في مبسوطه»، والقاضي بدر الأئمة، كذلك قال في «مجمع الروايات»، وكذا نقله في ( البحر ) عن ( المحيط ) ، ثم قال : وهذا كله يفيد أن التلفظ بها يكون بهذه العبارة: اللهم إني أريد إلخ، لا بنحو نويت أو أنوي، كما عليه عامة المتلفظين بالنيـة مـن عـامي وغيره، ولا يخفى أن سؤال التوفيق والقبول شيء آخر غير التلفظ بها، على أنه قد ذكر غير واحد من مشايخنا في وجه ما ذكره محمد في كتاب الحج، أن الحج لما كان يمتد ويقع فيه العوارض والموانع وهو عبادة عظيمة يحصل بأفعال شاقة، استحب طلب التيسير والتسهيل من الله تعالى، ولم يشرع مثل هذا الدعاء في الصلاة، لأن أداءها في وقت يسير. انتهى. وهو صريح في نفي قياس الصلاة على الحج. انتهى. وقد يكون الصوم مثل الحج لطول وقته ومشقته.
…the heart and strengthens it, and that is because the sunna was legislated to complete the obligatory, and mentioning it with the tongue is a reinforcement of the obligatory, so it becomes sunna. End. In (Al-Ikhtiyar Sharh al-Mukhtar), Muhammad ibn al-Hasan said: Intention with the heart is obligatory, and mentioning it with the tongue is sunna, and combining both is best, end. Al-Kamal ibn al-Humam said: Some of the memorizers (meaning Ibn Qayyim al-Jawziyya, as indicated by his statement, may Allah have mercy on him) said: It is not established from the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, by an authentic or even weak chain that he used to say at the beginning of prayer 'I pray such-and-such,' nor from any of the Companions or Successors; rather the transmitted report is that 'when he stood for prayer, he would say the takbir' (1), and this is an innovation. End. In 'Majma' al-Riwayat': Some disliked vocalizing the intention, because it is an innovation and deviation from what is done; some permitted it because it helps actualize the heart's action and cut off waswasa (whispers), and 'Umar only rebuked one who did it aloud, but whispering it is fine—as in Jami' al-Ahkam and Lawami' al-Ilham. End. So one who says that vocalizing the intention is sunna does not mean it is the sunna of the Prophet, but rather the sunna (practice) of some shaykhs, who chose it due to changing times and the many distractions on hearts after the time of the Companions and Successors.

Another note on how to vocalize it: He said in Al-Diraya: He should not say 'I intend such-and-such' because it would be a lie if he did not intend, and if he did intend it becomes a report about the actualized intention without need; rather he should say: 'O Allah, I want to pray such-and-such, so facilitate it for me and accept it from me,' as reported from Muhammad regarding the consecration for Hajj. End. In Al-Mabsut: He should not say 'I intended' because if he did not intend, he has lied, and if he intended after the intention, he has informed Allah of what is in his heart while He is already knowing, so it becomes [like] questioning; rather he should say: 'O Allah, I want to pray such-and-such prayer, so facilitate it for me and accept it from me,' so that it is a supplication for acceptance and a request for facilitation from Allah in achieving his goal. Similarly stated by Qadi Sadr al-Islam in his Mabsut, and Qadi Badr al-A'imma, and likewise in Majma' al-Riwayat, and similarly in Al-Bahr from Al-Muhit, then he said: All this indicates that vocalization should be with this expression: 'O Allah, I want...' etc., not with 'I intended' or 'I intend' as is the common practice of the general public and others when vocalizing the intention. And it is not hidden that asking for success and acceptance is something other than vocalizing the intention, although more than one of our shaykhs have mentioned, regarding what Muhammad mentioned in the book of Hajj, that because Hajj is extended and involves obstacles and impediments, and it is a great act of worship involving strenuous actions, seeking facilitation and ease from Allah is recommended, but such a supplication was not prescribed in prayer because its performance is in a short time. End. And it is explicit in negating the analogy of prayer to Hajj. End. And fasting may be like Hajj due to its extended time and hardship.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof on the issue of vocalizing the intention (talfuz bi-niyyah) in acts of worship, particularly prayer. It begins by stating the mainstream Hanafi view that intention with the heart is obligatory (fard), while mentioning it with the tongue is sunna, as stated by Muhammad ibn al-Hasan (Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani). However, the passage then presents a critical view from Al-Kamal ibn al-Humam, citing Ibn Qayyim al-Jawziyyah, that there is no authentic or even weak report from the Prophet (peace be upon him) or the Companions that they used to say 'I pray such-and-such' at the start of prayer; the only transmitted action is that he would say the takbir. Therefore, vocalizing the intention is an innovation (bid'ah). The passage reconciles these views by noting that later scholars (from among the mashayikh) chose it as a practice (sunnah) due to the changing times and increased distractions, not as a prophetic sunna. Then, the passage discusses the proper phrasing: one should not say 'nawaytu' (I intended) because it may be a lie or unnecessary report; instead one should phrase it as a supplication: 'O Allah, I want to pray such-and-such, so facilitate it for me and accept it from me.' This dual approach—considering the vocalization as a recommended practice but cautioning against specific wording—reflects the Hanafi school's pragmatic balancing of legal principle and practical need.

Key Terms
التلفظ بالنية (al-talfuz bi'l-niyyah): Uttering the intention verbally before beginning an act of worship.
النية (al-niyyah): The intention in the heart to perform a specific act of worship for Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did the Prophet (peace be upon him) say when starting prayer?
A) He said 'I pray such-and-such'.
B) He said the takbir.
C) He said 'O Allah, I want to pray'.
D) He was silent.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that intention by heart is fard, vocalization is a later recommended practice (sunnat al-mashayikh), and the preferred phrasing is a supplication, not a declaration.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate
10 min
Intention of Following the Imam for the Muqtadi
Conditions of takbirat al-ihram – intention of following

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the required intention for the muqtadi, identify acceptable wordings, and evaluate the dispute regarding 'intending the imam's prayer'.

Core Idea
The muqtadi must explicitly intend to follow the imam; mere waiting or intending the imam's prayer does not suffice.

In congregational prayer, the follower (muqtadi) must have an intention that ties his prayer to the imam's. This lesson unpacks the fourth condition for the validity of the opening takbir: niyyat al-mutaba'ah. We examine the required formulation, the dispute among early Hanafis, and practical scenarios like mistaken identity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the intention of following the imam at the time of takbirat al-ihram. The ruling is that it is a condition (shart) for the validity of the takbir, thereby making it wajib for the muqtadi.
Not a rukn; its omission invalidates the takbir, not the entire prayer's intention.
2
List the acceptable wordings
From the sources: 'I intend to follow the imam in this prayer' or 'I intend to start the imam's prayer' are acceptable. Adding 'following him' is recommended. Mere waiting does not suffice.
The wording 'I intended the imam's prayer' is disputed.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Some scholars (al-Sarakhsi, al-Kirmani, al-Jallabi) accept 'I intended the imam's prayer' as sufficient, arguing it implies following. The majority reject this, as it is a designation of the imam's prayer, not an expression of following.
The thamarah: if one uses the disputed wording, the prayer may be invalid according to the majority.
4
Apply to cases of mistaken identity
If the muqtadi intends to follow the imam in the mihrab (position) but misidentifies the person, it is valid. If he specifically intends to follow Zayd and it turns out to be Amr, it is invalid.
Consideration is given to the intended object (the imam in the mihrab), not the name.
5
Distinguish the imam's intention
The imam does not need to intend imamate for his prayer to be valid for followers, as he is praying for himself. This is evidenced by oath scenarios.
The condition applies only to the muqtadi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

انتهى، قلت: وفيه رد علــى مـا تقدم من أنه لا يقول نويت انتهى. وفي ( الفتاوى الظهيرية : ينبغي للمقتدي، أن لا يعين الإمام عند كثرة القوم، وكذا في صلاة الجنازة عند كثرة القوم ينبغي أن لا يعين الميت، ولا عرفه بالإشارة، فلغت التسمية. وكذا لو كان آخر الصفوف لا يرى شخصه، فنوى الاقتداء بالإمام القائم في المحراب الذي هو زيد، فإذا هو عمرو، جاز أيضاً، انتهى. وقال في البحر » : أطلق أي صاحب «الكنز» في اشتراط نية المتابعة لقوله: والمقتدي ينوي المتابعة أيضاً. وتعين الفرض فيشمل الجمعة. لكن في ( الذخيرة ) و « قاضي خان»، لو نوى الجمعة ولم ينو الاقتداء بالإمام، فإنـه يجوز؛ لأن الجمعة لا تكون إلا مع الإمام. انتهى. قلت: فكذلك العيد. انتهى. ثـم قـال: وقيـد بالمقتدي لأن الإمام لا يشترط في صحة اقتداء الرجال به نية الإمامة، لأنه منفرد في حق نفسه، ألا يرى أنه لو حلف أن لا يؤم أحداً فصلى خلفه جماعة، لم يحنث لأن شرط الحنث أن يقصد الإمامة، ولم يوجد بخلاف ما لو حلف أن لا يؤم فلاناً، لرجل بعينه، فصلى ونوى أن يؤم الناس، فصلى ذلك الرجل مع الناس خلفه، فإنه يحنث، وإن لم يعلم به، لأنه لما نوى الناس، دخـل هـذا الواحد.
And the fourth condition for the validity of the opening takbir is the intention of following (the imam) along with the intention of the original prayer for the follower. As for the common intention, we have already discussed it. As for the specific intention for the follower, it is because corruption may affect him from his imam, so he must commit to it. Concerning how to make this intention, it is stated in al-Muhit: He intends the prescribed prayer of the time and following the imam in it, or he intends to start the imam's prayer, or he intends to follow the imam in his prayer. If he only intends to follow him without more, some say it is not sufficient, but the correct view is that it is sufficient because he makes himself a follower of the imam absolutely. Following in every respect is realized only when he prays what the imam prays. If he says: 'I intended the imam's prayer,' it is not sufficient because that specifies the imam's prayer and is not following him. This is stated in Majma' al-Riwayat and similarly in al-Diraya from al-Muhit from Mabsut of Shaykh al-Islam. In Sharh al-Tahawi, if he intended the imam's prayer, it suffices and serves as two intentions; this is the view of al-Sarakhsi, al-Kirmani, and al-Jallabi. Some say: When he waits for the imam's takbir and then says takbir after him, that suffices for the intention of following because his waiting indicates intention of following. The correct view is that he does not become a follower by mere waiting, because waiting could be for following or out of habit. So unless he intends following, he does not become a follower. They said: If he wants to make it easy for himself, he says, 'I have started the imam's prayer,' and that suffices. Imam Zahir al-Din al-Marghinani said: He should add to this and say, 'following him.' In Fatawa Qadi Khan, he said: 'I intend to pray with the imam what the imam prays.' I say: This refutes what was previously stated that he should not say 'I intended.' In al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya: The follower should not specify the imam when there is a large crowd; similarly in the funeral prayer when there is a large crowd, he should not specify the deceased. There is consensus that intending the number of rak'ats is not required, whether one is a follower or not. Al-Kamal said: He should intend the imam standing in the mihrab, whoever it may be. Even if he does not think of him as Zayd or Amr, his following is valid. If he intends following the imam standing there, thinking he is Zayd but he is Amr, his following is valid because consideration is given to what he intended, not what he saw. He intended to follow the imam, unlike if he intended to follow Zayd and it turned out to be Amr, then it is not valid because consideration is given to what he intended. Similarly in fasting: if he intended to make up Thursday but it was another day, it is not valid; but if he intended to make up what he owes of fasting, thinking it was Thursday but it was another, it is valid. Even if he saw the person and intended to follow this imam who is Zayd, but it turned out to be another, it is valid because he identified him by pointing, so the name is disregarded. Similarly, if he was in the last row and did not see his person, and intended to follow the imam standing in the mihrab who is Zayd, but it turned out to be Amr, it is also valid. End. In al-Bahr, he said: The author of al-Kanz made it absolute in requiring the intention of following, saying: The follower must also intend to follow. And specifying the prescribed prayer includes Jumu'ah. But in al-Dhakhira and Qadi Khan: If he intended Jumu'ah but did not intend to follow the imam, it is valid because Jumu'ah is only with the imam. End. I say: Similarly for Eid. End. Then he said: It is restricted to the follower because the imam does not need to intend imamate for the validity of men following him, since he is alone in his own right. Do you not see that if he swore not to lead anyone and then some people prayed behind him, he would not be guilty of breaking the oath because the condition for breaking it is to intend leading, which is not present. Unlike if he swore not to lead a specific person, then prayed intending to lead people, and that person prayed with the people behind him, then he breaks the oath even if he did not know about him, because when he intended the people, this one person was included.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage discusses the fourth condition for the validity of the opening takbir (takbirat al-ihram): the intention of following the imam (niyyat al-mutaba'ah) for the one praying behind an imam (al-muqtadi). The author explains that the muqtadi must intend to follow the imam in addition to the general intention of prayer. The reason is that the muqtadi's prayer is linked to the imam's, and any corruption in the imam's prayer affects him. The text covers various formulations of this intention: intending the prescribed prayer and following the imam, intending to start the imam's prayer, or intending to follow the imam in his prayer. There is a discussion about whether saying 'I intended the imam's prayer' suffices; some early Hanafi scholars (like Sarakhsi, Kirmani, Jallabi) consider it sufficient, while others require explicit following. The passage also deals with the scenario of waiting for the imam's takbir: mere waiting does not constitute intention of following unless one actually intends it. For ease, one may say 'I started the imam's prayer' or add 'following him' as recommended by al-Marghinani. In large crowds, the muqtadi should not specify the imam by name; similarly in funeral prayer, he should not specify the deceased. Intending the number of rak'ats is not required by consensus. The discussion also covers cases of mistaken identity: if the muqtadi intends to follow the imam in the mihrab but mistakenly thinks it is Zayd when it is Amr, the prayer is valid because the intent is on the position, not the person. However, if he specifically intended to follow Zayd and it turned out to be Amr, it is invalid. The passage concludes by distinguishing the muqtadi from the imam: the imam does not need to intend imamate for the validity of others following him, as evidenced by oaths.

Key Terms
نية المتابعة (niyyat al-mutaba'ah): The intention of following the imam in prayer, required for the validity of congregational prayer for the follower
المقتدي (al-muqtadi): The person who prays behind an imam, following him in the prayer
التحريمة (al-tahrimah): The opening takbir (saying Allahu Akbar) that makes the prayer sacred and prohibits unrelated actions

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view, which of the following wordings for the muqtadi's intention is NOT acceptable?
A) I intend to follow the imam in this prayer
B) I intend to start the imam's prayer
C) I intended the imam's prayer
D) I intend to pray with the imam what he prays

Key Takeaway
The muqtadi must explicitly intend to follow the imam; mere indirect reference or waiting does not suffice.

Specifying the Fard Prayer in Intention – Basic Ruling
Initial ruling on ta'yin al-fard.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that specification of the fard prayer is wajib and apply it to cases of forgotten intention.

Core Idea
Specifying which fard prayer you intend is wajib; a vague intention risks invalidity.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah explains the fifth condition for the validity of the opening takbir: ta'yin al-fard, specifying the prescribed prayer. The author clarifies why this is necessary and addresses scenarios where the intention is forgotten or incomplete. We will examine the basic rule and its application to the imam, follower, and individual worshipper.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and its ruling
The text states that specifying the fard prayer is a condition for the validity of the opening takbir. The hukm level is wajib.
This is not a pillar but a condition.
2
Analyze the case of mistaken completion
If someone intends a fard, forgets, and completes it as nafl, it counts as fard because the intention at the start is what matters.
This illustrates the principle of al-fard al-musqit.
3
Note the scope of specification
Specification applies to the imam, the follower, and the one praying alone because of the multiplicity of fard prayers (ada and qada).
Everyone must specify.
4
Understand why vague intentions fail
Merely intending 'Zuhr' without specifying whether it's the current one or a makeup does not suffice, as one may have missed prayers. The only exception is a minority view from al-Attabi.
The default is that it does not suffice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وَ) الخامس من شروط صحة التحريمة: ( تَعيينُ الفَرْضِ) في ابتداء الشروع، حتى لو نوى فرضاً، وشرع فيه، ثم نسي، فظنه تطوعاً فأتمه على ظن أنه تطوع، فهو فرض مسقط، لأن النية المعتبرة إنما يشترط قرانها للجزء الأول. وكذا عكسه يكون تطوعاً، ولا يشترط نية أعداد الركعات حتى لو نوى الفجر أربعاً أو الظهر ثلاثاً، وأتمه على الوجه المطلوب، صحت واشتراط التعين يشمل الإمام والمقتدي والمنفرد لتزاحم الفروض أداء وقضاء، فلابد من تعيين ما يريد، لأن أسبابها مختلفة، وباختلافها يختلف الواجب، فينوي ظهر اليوم، أو ظهر الوقت مثلاً ، فلو نوى الظهر مثلاً ولم يضف إليه مـا يخصصه، لا تجزئه، لأنه ربما يكون عليه فائتة، فلا يتعين إلا أنه في فتاوى العتابي» قال: الأصح أنه يجزئه. انتهى. ولو نوى بلفظ الفرض فقط، لا يجزئه أيضاً، لأن الفرائض متنوعة، أمـا لـو نـوى فرض الوقت في الوقت، أجزأه إلا في الجمعة، كما سنذكره، وخارج الوقت، لا؛ لأن بعـد خـروج الوقت، فرض الوقتِ العصر، لا الظهر. كذا في ( الدراية » ، فإن خرج ونسيه، لا يجزئه في الصحيح، كما في «الفتح» والأولى أن ينوي ظهر اليوم سواء كان الوقت خارجاً أو لا، لاختلاف الفروض، وفي «جامع الكردري : ينوي فرض الجمعة، ولا ينوي فرض الوقت لأنه مختلف فيه، وإذا جمع بين فائتة وحاضرة، لا يصير شارعاً في واحدة منهما، كنية العصر والظهر في وقت العصر وفي المنتقى» إن كان في الوقت سعة يصير شارعاً في الظهر وفي « الخلاصة»: إن نوى مكتوبتين فائتتين كانت للأولى منهما، وعلله في ( المحيط» بأن الثانية لا تجوز إلا بعد قضاء الأولى. قال صاحب « البحر » : وهو إنما يتم لو كان الترتيب بينهما واجباً. انتهى. قلت: وهـو محمل ذلك، لأنـه قال في خير مطلوب: لو نوى فرضين، لا يصير شارعاً في أحدهما ، فيحمل على ما سقط ترتيبه، إذ ليس أحدهما أولى من الآخر في التقدير توفيقاً بين النفلين. انتهى. ولو جمع بين فرض ونفل يصير شارعاً في الفرض عند أبي يوسف، لأن الفرض أقوى من النفل، فلا يعارضه فتلغو نية النفل، وتبقى
And the fifth condition for the validity of the opening takbir is the specification of the prescribed prayer at the beginning of starting. So if he intends a fard (prescribed prayer) and starts it, then forgets and thinks it is a supererogatory prayer, and completes it thinking it is supererogatory, it is a fard that drops his obligation. This is because the valid intention is required to accompany the first part. Similarly, the opposite would be supererogatory. And it is not required to intend the number of rak'ats. So if he intends Fajr as four or Zuhr as three, and completes it as required, it is valid. The requirement of specification includes the imam, the follower, and the one praying alone because of the multiplicity of fard prayers, both on time and makeup. So he must specify what he wants, because their causes are different, and the obligatory differs according to them. So he intends, for example, the Zuhr of today or the Zuhr of the time. If he intends just 'Zuhr' without adding something that specifies it, it does not suffice, because maybe he has a missed prayer. It is not specified. However, in Fatawa al-Attabi, he said: The correct view is that it does suffice. End. And if he intends only the word 'fard', it also does not suffice because fard prayers are diverse. As for if he intends 'the fard of the time' within the time, it suffices except for Jumu'ah, as we will mention. Outside the time, it does not suffice because after the time exits, the fard of the time is 'Asr, not Zuhr. This is in al-Diraya. If the time has left and he forgot, it does not suffice in the correct view, as in al-Fath. It is better to intend 'Zuhr of today' whether the time is in or out, due to the diversity of obligations. In Jami' al-Kardari: He intends the fard of Jumu'ah, not the fard of the time, because it is disputed. And if he combines a missed prayer and a present one, he does not become a starter of either. For example, intending 'Asr and Zuhr in 'Asr time. In al-Muntaqa: If there is ample time, he becomes a starter of Zuhr. In al-Khulasa: If he intends two missed obligatory prayers, it is considered for the first of them. And he explained in al-Muhit that the second is not valid except after performing the first. The author of al-Bahr said: This only stands if order (tartib) is obligatory between them. End. I say: This is the interpretation, because he said in Khayr Mathlub: If he intends two fard prayers, he does not become a starter of either. It is interpreted as when order has been dropped, as neither has priority over the other, harmonizing the two supererogatory? End. And if he combines a fard and a supererogatory, he becomes a starter of the fard according to Abu Yusuf, because the fard is stronger than the supererogatory, so the supererogatory intention is overruled and only the fard remains.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage discusses the fifth condition for the validity of the opening takbir: specifying which fard prayer one intends (ta'yin al-fard). The reason is that there are multiple obligations (ada and qada), so the worshipper must know which one he is performing. The ruling applies to the imam, the follower, and the one praying alone. The text gives examples: if one forgets and mistakenly completes as supererogatory, it still counts as fard because intention at the start is what matters. Intending the number of rak'ats is not required. The text warns against vague intentions: merely intending 'Zuhr' does not suffice because it might not specify whether it is the current Zuhr or a makeup; however, there is a minority view from al-Attabi that it suffices. Intending 'fard' alone also does not suffice. Within the time, intending 'fard of the time' works except for Jumu'ah. Outside the time, it does not suffice. The recommended wording is 'Zuhr of today' to cover all possibilities. For Jumu'ah, one should intend 'fard of Jumu'ah' specifically. The text also deals with combining multiple intentions: if one intends two fard prayers (e.g., a makeup and current prayer), he does not validly start either; if he intends two missed prayers, it counts for the first one only if order is obligatory. If he combines a fard and a nafl, according to Abu Yusuf, the fard intention prevails.

Key Terms
تعيين الفرض (ta'yin al-fard): Specifying which fard prayer one is performing in the intention
الفرض المسقط (al-fard al-musqit): A fard prayer that fulfills the obligation even if mistakenly completed as nafl; it is 'dropping' the duty
فرض الوقت (fard al-waqt): The prescribed prayer due at the current time

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a person intends just 'Zuhr' without any specification during the Zuhr time, and has no missed prayers?
A) Valid, because the time specifies it.
B) Valid, according to al-Attabi but not the majority.
C) Invalid, because it does not suffice even if no missed prayers.
D) Invalid, unless he also intends the number of rak'ats.

Key Takeaway
Specification of the fard prayer in intention is wajib; vague intentions like 'Zuhr' or 'fard' alone are insufficient.

Specifying 'Fard al-Waqt' and the Recommended Wording
Clarification of 'fard al-waqt' specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between valid and invalid specifications using 'fard al-waqt' inside and outside the time.

Core Idea
'Fard of the time' works inside the time but not outside; 'Zuhr of today' is safest.

This passage continues the discussion of specification, focusing on the expression 'fard al-waqt' (the fard of the time). The author explains when it suffices and when it does not, and recommends a more precise wording. We will also see the specific rule for Jumu'ah.

Guided Path
1
Understand 'fard al-waqt' inside the time
If one intends 'the fard of the time' while the time is present, it suffices because it refers to the current obligatory prayer.
Exception: Jumu'ah requires explicit mention.
2
Analyze 'fard al-waqt' outside the time
Outside the time, this expression does not suffice because the 'fard of the time' would be the next prayer, not the one intended.
E.g., intending 'fard of the time' for Zuhr after 'Asr time begins would refer to 'Asr.
3
Note the recommendation
The text recommends intending 'Zuhr of today' whether in time or out, to avoid any ambiguity.
This is the safest formulation.
4
Apply the rule for Jumu'ah
For Jumu'ah, one should intend 'fard of Jumu'ah' specifically, not 'fard of the time', because the latter is disputed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Combining Multiple Intentions in Prayer
Sub-rulings on combining intentions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which intention prevails when combining fard and nafl, and when combining two fard prayers.

Core Idea
Combining fard and nafl: the fard prevails; combining two fards: neither is valid if both are intended simultaneously.

This final passage addresses scenarios where the worshipper attempts to combine multiple obligations or a fard and a supererogatory prayer in one intention. The author discusses the views of different Hanafi authorities and the conditions under which combining is possible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule for combining fard and nafl
According to Abu Yusuf, if someone intends both a fard and a nafl, only the fard intention prevails because it is stronger.
The nafl intention is overruled.
2
Understand the rule for combining two fards
If one intends two fard prayers (e.g., a current and a missed prayer), he does not become a starter of either, unless there is ample time and order is not obligatory.
This is a complex area with multiple views.
3
Note the exception for ample time
Al-Muntaqa states that if there is ample time, intending a missed and a current prayer results in starting the current prayer (Zuhr).
This depends on the obligation of order (tartib).
4
Apply the rule for two missed prayers
If one intends two missed prayers, it counts for the first one because order between them is obligatory.
The second is not valid until the first is performed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
